            }
            return "blank";

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="e2ede9d4900c4f1784117ef738efc464">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'ab8e92f8-a109-47de-8012-1181932b281f'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:jiajiahan@xmu.edu.cn"><span>jiajiahan@xmu.edu.cn</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:wangcp@xmu.edu.cn"><span>wangcp@xmu.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Wang/Cuiping">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span></div>
         </div>
      </div>
   </div>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d8813751" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Vanadate materials are promising for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to their low cost, high capacity, and high power characteristics enabled by vanadium's multiple oxidation states. However, their development is hindered by poor conductivity, suboptimal high-rate performance, and limited cycle life. In this work, a layered structure modification strategy involving Ca/F co-doping in sodium vanadate Na<sub>2</sub>CaV<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F (CVF) is proposed to address these issues. Through a combination of experiments and density functional theory calculations, it is demonstrated that Ca/F synergies enhance the Na layer spacing in CVF, resulting in reduced crystal water content and volume shrinkage compared to Na<sub>2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub> (NVO). Additionally, Ca/F incorporation significantly mitigates the diffusion potential of Na<sup>+</sup> within the material framework. The unmodified CVF sample exhibits a high reversible capacity of 220 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and an excellent rate capacity of 65.78 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 400&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, the cathode material maintains a capacity of up to 138 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 200&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and retains 104.88 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles within the voltage range of 1.5−4.0&nbsp;V. These findings enhance the understanding of the crystal structure of NVO cathode materials and pave the way for the rational design of high-quality vanadate cathodes for SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202401481-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202401481-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The rapid proliferation of portable electronic devices and electric vehicles has intensified the demand for energy storage systems that offer high power output and uncompromised safety.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0001_R_d8813743e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - iScience">1</a>-<a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0004_R_d8813743e424" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Joule">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), as the most representative energy storage technology, play a pivotal role in large-scale applications owing to their high energy density and extended cycle life. However, several critical factors, such as limited lithium resources, high production costs, and the use of potentially hazardous organic electrolytes constrain the further advancement of LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0005_R_d8813743e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a>-<a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0007_R_d8813743e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Energy Lett.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, in order to address these issues, Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) emerge as promising contenders.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0008_R_d8813743e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0009_R_d8813743e434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Electrochim. Acta">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The success of SIBs hinges greatly on the quality of their cathode materials. In recent years, vanadyl oxides and vanadate have emerged as promising options for use as positive electrode materials in LIBs, garnering significant interest from researchers and industry professionals alike.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0009_R_d8813743e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Electrochim. Acta">9</a>, <a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0010_R_d8813743e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Mater. Chem. A">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These vanadium-based compounds, such as V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0011_R_d8813743e451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Alloys Compd.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> VO<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0012_R_d8813743e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Mater. Chem. A">12</a>-<a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0014_R_d8813743e459" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Alloys Compd.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaV<sub>3</sub>O<sub>8</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0015_R_d8813743e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Eng. J.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>5</sub>V<sub>12</sub>O<sub>32</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0016_R_d8813743e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Mater. Res. Bull.">16</a>-<a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0018_R_d8813743e481" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Fuels">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and more, boast layered or tunnel structures that offer ample room for the seamless insertion/extraction of single/multiple ions, creating ample storage capacity for cations.</p>
         
         <p>Similar to the extensively studied Li<sub>1+</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>V<i><sub>n</sub></i>O<i><sub>m</sub></i>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0019_R_d8813743e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Alloys Compd.">19</a>, <a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0020_R_d8813743e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Electrochem. Commun.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the promising Na<sub>1+</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>V<i><sub>n</sub></i>O<i><sub>m</sub></i> is also seen as an ideal candidate for LIBs due to its high capacity, attributed to the multiple oxidation states of vanadium, and impressive power capabilities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0021_R_d8813743e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">21</a>, <a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0022_R_d8813743e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Sci. Technol.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite its straightforward synthesis process, practical application reveals some drawbacks such as poor electronic conductivity, significant volume changes during sodium ion insertion/extraction, rapid decay of cycle capacity, and subpar rate performance during charge and discharge. To enhance its performance, common methods of improvement include doping,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0023_R_d8813743e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> nanostructuring,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0024_R_d8813743e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chin. Chem. Lett.">24</a>, <a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0025_R_d8813743e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Nano">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> surface coating<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0026_R_d8813743e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">26</a>, <a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0027_R_d8813743e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Mater. Chem. A">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the development of heterogeneous structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0028_R_d8813743e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Small">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recent research indicates that metallic interlayer ions (M<i><sub>x</sub></i>V<i><sub>n</sub></i>O<i><sub>m</sub></i>, M: metal ions) can enhance the stability of layered vanadium oxides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0029_R_d8813743e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> incorporating ions such as Li<sup>+</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup> and Cu<sup>2+</sup>, as shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). While structural modification of vanadate often involves doping at sodium sites or expanding layer spacing through large ion doping, the impact of small ion doping remains underexplored.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0030_R_d8813743e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Ceram. Int.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Among the array of intricate transition metal oxides, the monoclinic vanadate AV<i><sub>n</sub></i>O<i><sub>m</sub></i><sub>-</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>•H<sub>2</sub>O, where A represents intercalated ions, stands out as a highly promising cathode for batteries due to its impressive theoretical capacity based on a one-electron transfer reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0031_R_d8813743e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nat. Commun.">31</a>-<a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0034_R_d8813743e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Sci. China Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this study, we propose a straightforward hydrothermal self-assembly approach to fabricate the Na<sub>2</sub>CaV<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F (CVF) cathode, which enhances the structure through doping substitutions at both sodium and oxygen sites to enhance its electrochemical performance. Our findings reveal that the incorporation of an appropriate amount of Ca/F co-doping successfully enhances the spacing between Na layers, thereby mitigating volume contraction effects during charge and discharge processes, while also reducing diffusion barriers and charge transfer impedance. The resulting cathode material exhibits a remarkable capacity of up to 138 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 200&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and retains a capacity of 104.88 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles within the voltage range of 1.5−4.0&nbsp;V.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202401481-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202401481-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The layered CVF cathode material was prepared through a streamlined one-step hydrothermal technique, with subsequent annealing. The detailed synthesis process is visually depicted in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0001">1</a>. Furthermore, as evidenced in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) the formation of the gel-like precursor is achieved through the process of hydrothermal self-assembly.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202401481-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/19931c72-7472-47c2-91b0-c214a45254b4/aenm202401481-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/19931c72-7472-47c2-91b0-c214a45254b4/aenm202401481-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0fe1a57f-fe0b-4cfe-9b9c-5425f00df0e2/aenm202401481-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/19931c72-7472-47c2-91b0-c214a45254b4/aenm202401481-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202401481-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202401481" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic diagram of the synthesis of CVF samples via hydrothermal reaction.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The morphology and microstructure of NVO, CVO, NVF and CVF are examined using SEM technology, as depicted in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0002">2a–d</a>. The SEM images revealed the well-defined rod-like morphology of the pristine NVO sample, with a length ranging from 1 to 2 um (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0002">2a</a>). Upon the introduction of F, the size decreased, showcasing a lamellar morphology (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0002">2b</a>), while the addition of Ca resulted in a transition from rod-like to lamellar morphology (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0002">2c</a>). Co-doping of F and Ca led to a lamellar morphology (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0002">2d</a>). The layered morphology of doped crystals increases the specific surface area, thus providing more active sites for redox reactions. Notably, the morphology of the doped samples remained largely intact compared to NVO after 100 cycles at 200&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), underscoring the role of Ca and F doping in reducing morphology breakage. To determine the optimal doping levels of Ca and F on the sodium and oxygen sites, CVO and NVF with three different concentrations were fabricated using the same method. SEM images of Ca and F with various doping amounts and the morphology of their optimal doping levels after cycling are illustrated in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202401481-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3b313377-48be-4761-9408-5a22fc143260/aenm202401481-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3b313377-48be-4761-9408-5a22fc143260/aenm202401481-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0b0098c3-0789-4d7b-8de5-a3a86f1adf6a/aenm202401481-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3b313377-48be-4761-9408-5a22fc143260/aenm202401481-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202401481-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202401481" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images of a) NVO, b) CVO, c) NVF, and d) CVF.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The detailed structure of the CVF samples is further scrutinized utilizing TEM and HRTEM. The TEM image (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0003">3a,b</a>) corroborated the lamellar morphology observed in the SEM image. The HRTEM image of the CVF (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0003">3c</a>) unveiled that the lattice spacing of the CVF sample (white pane selection area) is 1.017&nbsp;nm, corresponding to (110) lattice planes. The lattice spacing is found to expand post-doping (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0003">3f</a>). In order to demonstrate the significant expansion effect of Ca-F co-doping on lattice spacing, Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information) present the HRTEM spacing of CVO and NVF samples. The results indicate that <i>d</i><sub>ca</sub> = 0.9978&nbsp;nm, <i>d</i><sub>f</sub> = 0.8711&nbsp;nm, which is substantially higher than CVF (<i>d</i> = 0.674&nbsp;nm). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0003">3d,e</a> is the TEM image of the NVO, and the morphology and size are consistent with the SEM image of NVO. Simultaneously, we disassembled the entire battery after 50 cycles and characterized the positive electrode material. SEM analysis revealed that the battery electrode sheet remained intact and exhibited a relatively regular initial morphology after 50 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a–c</a>, Supporting Information), indicating no fracture. Further, HRTEM analysis showed a lattice spacing of 0.921&nbsp;nm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7g</a>, Supporting Information), maintaining a large layer spacing. EDS analysis confirmed the presence of Na, V, O, Ca, and F in the crystal structure after 50 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7j–o</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting that the composition remained consistent.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202401481-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2e71d9f5-52b1-4fb0-a74e-8db8f11210cd/aenm202401481-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2e71d9f5-52b1-4fb0-a74e-8db8f11210cd/aenm202401481-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/aa68593a-28b1-44e9-93a3-825facab9481/aenm202401481-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2e71d9f5-52b1-4fb0-a74e-8db8f11210cd/aenm202401481-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202401481-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202401481" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) TEM images of CVF; c) HRTEM images of CVF; d,e) TEM images of NVO; f) HRTEM images of NVO; g–l) EDS mapping images of CVF; m–p) EDS mapping images of NVO.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The SAED image and FFT patterns in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0003">3c,f</a> indicate that CVF and NVO are single crystals. To further probe the crystallinity of the CVF sample used for TEM experiments, we conducted thorough structural characterization using SAED (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). Due to the large size of the particles (mostly micrometers), which are too thick for the electron beam to penetrate, we focused analysis on the relatively thin edge regions. SAED patterns acquired from four different edge sites of a single particle confirmed that all these domains possess the same crystal orientation, indicating the single-crystal nature of the particle. This observation, combined with the analysis of three different particles in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), provides strong evidence that the CVF particles used in the TEM experiments are single crystalline.</p>
         
         <p>Additionally, EDS test was employed to qualitatively analyze the element distribution in the samples. The EDS spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0003">3g–l</a>) confirmed the presence of Na, V, Ca, F, and O in the CVF sample, with the element contents detailed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). And the element contents of CVO, NVF, NVO samples from EDS detailed in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3–S5</a> (Supporting Information). To determine the precise composition of Ca and F doping within the crystal, inductively coupled plasma (ICP) and ion chromatography (IC) analyses were performed (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). These analyses revealed that the crystal contained 12.11% Na, 0.18% Ca, 33.6% O, and 1.72% F. This corresponds to an actual doping ratio of 1.5% Ca and 14.2% F relative to Na. The lower actual doping amount of Ca ions is attributed to their larger ionic radius, which likely limits their incorporation into the crystal lattice.</p>
         
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0003">3m–p</a> depicts the EDS mapping images of NVO, revealing the distribution of elements within the sample. Simultaneously, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information) presents line scan results for the individual elements, demonstrating the uniform distribution of Ca and F throughout the bulk material. These results confirm that the doping process effectively incorporates Ca and F without any noticeable surface segregation or atomic-scale recombination.</p>
         
         <p>The crystal structure of NVO, CVO, NVF, and CVF is examined through XRD analysis, as elucidated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0004">4a</a>. The impact of introducing Ca and F dopants to the crystal structure was thoroughly investigated, affirming the preservation of the crystalline structure of pure NVO upon appropriate Ca and F incorporation (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). The XRD patterns of the four samples exhibited striking similarities, which corresponds to PDF card number #01-072-2272. The distinct diffraction peaks displayed high intensity, reflective of profound crystallinity. Specifically, the primary diffraction peaks at 12.84°, 23.1°, 25.9°, 28°, 29.9°, 40.15°, and 50.74° denoted reflections of the crystal plane (110), (111), (220), (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7c333267-fc59-4d10-9fad-adbdc7c0d8ee/aenm202401481-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202401481:aenm202401481-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202401481-math-0001.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mover accent="true">
                     <mn>3</mn>
                     <mo>¯</mo>
                  </mover>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{\bar{3}}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>11), (130), (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/57fdd85e-16c8-4a80-8a0a-c500dacf8988/aenm202401481-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202401481:aenm202401481-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202401481-math-0002.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mover accent="true">
                     <mn>4</mn>
                     <mo>¯</mo>
                  </mover>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{4}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>02) and (042), respectively.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202401481-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/cdea70ae-a4f9-486d-b90e-aab9a97be0e6/aenm202401481-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/cdea70ae-a4f9-486d-b90e-aab9a97be0e6/aenm202401481-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3f51ea43-55ee-4a0a-8bee-9b284009e869/aenm202401481-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/cdea70ae-a4f9-486d-b90e-aab9a97be0e6/aenm202401481-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202401481-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202401481" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns of NVO, CVO, NVF and CVF samples. b) Retrieved refinement of CVF sample. c) Energy of CVF and CVF(Ca-F). d) FT-IR spectrogram of NVO, CVO, NVF and CVF samples. e) TG curves of NVO, CVO, NVF, and CVF samples. f) UV–Vis spectroscopy of spectrum of CVF and NVO samples. g) XPS spectrum of CVF sample. h) High-resolution XPS spectrum of V<sub>2p</sub> for CVF sample. i) XPS spectrum of CVF and NVO samples at low energy level.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The Rietveld refinement of the CVF is shown in&nbsp;Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0004">4b</a> and the detailed structural information of NVO is shown in&nbsp;Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information). The CVF and NVO materials possess the typical monoclinic structure with C 12/C 1 space group&nbsp;with R&nbsp;below acceptable values (<i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> = 4.66%, <i>R</i><sub>p</sub> = 4.15% for CVF;&nbsp;<i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> = 4.15%, <i>R</i><sub>p</sub> = 4.15% for NVO). Detailed structural information of CVF and NVO derived from Rietveld refinement are shown in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9 and S10</a> (Supporting Information). The XRD pattern of CVF closely resembles that of pure NVO, indicating that the structural integrity is preserved after doping with Ca and F. The energy from DFT calculations (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0004">4c</a>) shows that the formation of Ca–F bonds leads to a higher energy state in the system, while the energy is lower when Ca and F do not form bonds. This indicates that after introducing Ca and F dopants, the system tends to avoid forming Ca─F bonds, and Ca and F disperse within the system.</p>
         
         <p>In order to ascertain the intricate details regarding the chemical bonding within the acquired samples, FT-IR spectra analyses were conducted on each specimen, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0004">4d</a>. Notably, all four samples exhibited identical infrared peaks, wherein the prominent band observed in the functional group segment at 3440 cm<sup>−1</sup> signifies the stretching vibration of the OH group, while the distinct band at 1630 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to the bending vibration of both crystal water and the OH group.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0018_R_d8813743e888" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Fuels">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the intense band at 949 cm<sup>−1</sup> is indicative of the asymmetric stretching vibration of the V═O bond. Furthermore, the bands observed at 554 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 759 cm<sup>−1</sup> signify the symmetric stretching vibrations of V─O─V,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0035_R_d8813743e900" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nanoscale">35</a>, <a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0036_R_d8813743e900" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> hence implying the presence of bound water in all samples. Interestingly, it is noted that the strong bands in the functional group regions of CVO, NVF, and CVF samples displayed a reduction in intensity at 3440 and 1630 cm<sup>−1</sup>, indicating a diminution in internal water content. This observation is congruent with the findings derived from the TG analysis. Research has demonstrated that the addition of water molecules as a stabilizing agent in the crystal structure has been shown to be effective,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0022_R_d8813743e908" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Sci. Technol.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> but the presence of interstitial water blocks the diffusion channel of sodium ions. Thus, maintaining the right amount of water may improve the electrochemical performance of the sample. In order to quantify the impact of F and Ca doping on the water content of the sample, TG analyses are conducted under N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere for four samples, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0004">4e</a>. Evidently, the water content in all four samples is different, with a noticeable decrease in water content observed in samples doped with F and Ca compared to samples doped with these elements separately. In particular, the internal water content of CVF is significantly lower than that of NVO, CVO and NVF. This suggests that the incorporation of F and Ca into the crystal structure has driven away a lot of water molecules. Further analysis from GCD tests, rate tests and EIS tests indicates that F and Ca can provide greater structural support than water and lower the diffusion barrier, thereby enhancing the electrochemical performance. UV–Vis spectroscopy was employed to investigate the bandgap values of both samples (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0004">4f</a>). The Ca and F co-doped sample exhibited a bandgap of 2.6&nbsp;eV, compared to 2.89&nbsp;eV for NVO. The introduction of Ca and F dopants leads to a decrease in the bandgap, lowering the energy barrier for electron migration and enhancing the kinetic properties.</p>
         
         <p>To delve into the chemical valency states of the constituent elements, XPS assays were conducted on the specimen, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0004">4g–i</a>. Peak patterns corresponding to C1s, V2p, V3p, O1s, and Na1s/Na2s are notably discernible in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0004">4g</a>. A high-resolution V2p spectrum shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0004">4h</a> revealed the presence of V in a mixed valency state of four and five. The peaks observed at 517.4 and 524.8&nbsp;eV are found to align with the binding energies of V 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and V 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively. Subsequent analysis of the XPS peaks through fitting yielded distinct and separate peaks corresponding to V<sup>5+</sup> and V<sup>4+</sup>. Noteworthy is the fact that the peaks observed at 517.4 and 524.8&nbsp;eV are attributable to V<sup>5+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and V<sup>5+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, while the comparatively less intense peaks at 515.8 and 523.4&nbsp;eV correspond to V<sup>4+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and V<sup>4+</sup>2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0037_R_d8813743e959" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Sci.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This finding is in alignment with the observations made for the NVO sample (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). Analysis of peak areas in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information) confirms that V primarily exists in the pentavalent state within CVF. For comparison, details of NVO are provided in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information). Furthermore, XPS analysis at low energies (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0004">4i</a>) reveals valence band positions of 1.10&nbsp;eV for CVF and 1.81&nbsp;eV for NVO. The co-doping of Ca and F results in a downward shift of the energy band as a whole, effectively lowering the energy barrier for electron migration.</p>
         
         <p>The crystal structure of the material undergoes modulations during charge and discharge processes, driven by the varying amounts of Na<sup>+</sup> within the lattice. Considering factors such as ionic radius similarity, electronegativity similarity, and charge balance between dopant atoms and host atoms, we can predict potential substitution sites. Specifically, Ca is expected to replace Na, while F is expected to replace O. To validate these doping locations, we examined several possible sites within the unit cell using DFT. Our calculations indicate that when Ca and F occupy the Ca2 and F2 sites, the system exhibits the lowest energy (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0005">5a</a>), suggesting its strong propensity to form a stable crystal structure.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202401481-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/92c5d1a6-a878-46db-a14c-cad9a8700cb1/aenm202401481-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/92c5d1a6-a878-46db-a14c-cad9a8700cb1/aenm202401481-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/758da87e-753d-4c6a-82f8-7c6ffd6dbbfa/aenm202401481-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/92c5d1a6-a878-46db-a14c-cad9a8700cb1/aenm202401481-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202401481-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202401481" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Total energy of CVF samples with Ca and F at different doping sites. b) Total density of states for NVO, CVO, NVF, and CVF. c) Initial cell volume comparison for NVO, CVO, NVF, and CVF. d) Volume changes in Na<sup>+</sup> half-full and empty states. e) Migration energy barriers of Na<sup>+</sup> in the NVO and CVF. f) Change in lattice constants of the cell under different charging states. g) Charge density difference and Bader charge as well as reaction energy of CVF. h) Structure transformation of CVF under different charging states.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Employing DFT, we have delved into the structural and kinetic attributes of NVO, CVO, NVF, and CVF with respect to the insertion and removal of Na<sup>+</sup>. Our analysis reveals that CVF showcases a theoretical bandgap value of 2.51&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0005">5b</a>), notably lower than that of NVO (2.91&nbsp;eV), which is similar to the bandgap value measured by experiment (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0004">4f</a>), indicating an enhancement in the electrical conductivity of the crystals upon the incorporation of Ca and Fe doping. Exploring cell volume of the samples in the full Na state (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0005">5c</a>), we find that larger dopant ion radius of Ca results in cell volume expansion, while smaller dopant ion radius of F leads to cell volume contraction. Interestingly, the co-doping of both Ca and F induces a more significant expansion of the cell volume, which aligns with the experimental findings (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0003">3c–f</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5 and S6</a>, Supporting Information). Further, upon assessing the volume alterations under varied charging states, it is evident that Ca and F doping mitigate the volume shrinkage observed in the later stages of charging, thus improving cyclic stability. Moreover, the incorporation of Ca and F doping is found to lower the diffusion barrier of Na<sup>+</sup>, thereby enhancing the diffusion rate along the c-axis direction within the crystal lattice (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0005">5e</a>). This boost in diffusion kinetics highlights the potential of these doped materials in facilitating efficient sodium ion movement during battery operations.</p>
         
         <p>Investigating the structural modifications of CVF following Ca and F doping under different charge states, we observe that with the gradual Na<sup>+</sup> detachment, the a-axis of the CVF cell structure extends and then contracts, the c-axis extends slightly while the b-axis continues to contract (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0005">5f</a>). To demonstrate that Ca plays a supporting role in the structure, we calculated the differential charge densities and Bader charges of Ca and Na in CVF. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0005">5g</a>, the Ca loses 1.56 electrons in bonding with O, which is more than the 0.66 electrons lost by the Na ion. In addition, the replacement of Na ions by Ca ions leads to a decrease in the energy of the system by 3.376&nbsp;eV, all of which indicate that Ca─O bonds are stronger than Na-O bonds, and the strong bonding of Ca─O allows it to be retained in the crystal lattice and thus reduces the volume contraction effect after Na removal. As depicted in the Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0005">5h</a>, it is evident that even after Ca and F doping, the crystal structure still exhibits a certain degree of contraction, which can be ascribed to the relatively low doping levels of Ca and F. As more Na<sup>+</sup> are extracted, the overall structure tends to contract further, indicating potential for enhancing its cyclic performance.</p>
         
         <p>The specimens were integrated into SIBs, with subsequent testing carried out to evaluate the rate performance and cycle performance, as depicted in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0006">6a,b</a>. The specific capacities of NVO are found to be 120 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 98 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 71 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 60 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>,43 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 25 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, 200&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and 400&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. It is evident that NVO exhibits suboptimal specific capacity at high current densities. Following the individual doping of F and Ca ion, there is a marked enhancement in the specific capacity of the battery at these current densities. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information) illustrates the rate performance and cycle performance resulting from the incorporation of varying concentrations of Ca and F doping. It has been conclusively demonstrated that the optimal doping levels of Ca and F stand at 2.5% and 15%, respectively.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202401481-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/93bc143b-16ba-4352-8d35-3debbbd075dc/aenm202401481-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/93bc143b-16ba-4352-8d35-3debbbd075dc/aenm202401481-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/dd58a71c-0ced-4a4e-82dd-731a1d3cfcce/aenm202401481-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/93bc143b-16ba-4352-8d35-3debbbd075dc/aenm202401481-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202401481-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202401481" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of the NVO, CVO, NVF and CVF for SIBs: a) Rate capability&nbsp;from 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> to 400&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>; b) Cyclic performance at 200&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>; c) GCD curves at 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>; d) GCD curves of CVF from 10 to 400&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>; e) GCD curves of CVF at 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>; f) Ex situ XRD patterns of CVF under different working states.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The electrochemical performance of NVF and CVO reveals specific capacities reaching 41 and 58 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, when subjected to a current density of 400&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. As the current density escalates from 10 to 400&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the CVF electrode displays specific capacities of 220, 175, 133, 109, 86, and 68 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> consecutively. Following 100 cycles at a current density of 200&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, CVF retains 76% of its initial capacity, outperforming NVO (68%), CVO (58%), and NVF (72%). Nonetheless, the rapid decay necessitates a continuous focus on enhancing the cyclic stability of sodium vanadate. Upon reverting to a current density of 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, CVF demonstrates a reversible capacity equivalent to its original value, in contrast to the diminished specific capacities observed in NVO, CVO, and NVF electrodes. The low electrochemical polarization and heightened reversibility of CVF distinctly distinguish its promising performance.</p>
         
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0006">6c–e</a> presents a detailed analysis of the GCD behavior of CVF at 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> for five cycles, the GCD curves of CVF at different current density and the first GCD curves of four samples at 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The GCD measurements conducted at 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> reveal the notably low electrochemical polarization and excellent reversibility. Furthermore, the GCD curves of the four samples exhibit closely aligned charging/discharging voltage platforms, delineating a uniform electrochemical behavior (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0006">6d</a>).</p>
         
         <p>In order to elaborate on the structural alterations of CVF at various states of charging/discharging subsequent to the extraction/insertion of sodium ions, Ex situ XRD measurements were executed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0006">6f</a>). It is noteworthy that all distinctive diffraction peaks exhibited a similar transitional pattern upon the initial extraction/insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>; however, additional diffraction peaks (Na<sub>2</sub>V<sub>6</sub>O<sub>16</sub>) are also discerned. The evolution of the structure during the second cycle and the amplification of specific angular ranges in the contour plot are illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0006">6f</a>. The apex value linked to the (110) crystal surface progressively shifts to a higher angle, leading to lattice contraction; conversely, the lattice undergoes expansion and substantial volume alterations during the discharge process. Moreover, upon reaching a 4&nbsp;V charge, a majority of the peaks vanished, the remaining diffraction peaks correspond exactly to the VO phase (PDF#01-77-2173), which signifies the complete removal of Na<sup>+</sup> at the 4&nbsp;V charging stage. The characteristic diffraction peak pertaining to the (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/392c94ac-ac52-4bba-ad79-017ec1102b90/aenm202401481-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202401481:aenm202401481-math-0003" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202401481-math-0003.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mover accent="true">
                     <mn>1</mn>
                     <mo>¯</mo>
                  </mover>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{1}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>11) crystal facet nearly dissipates post-charging, indicative of a crystal orientation preference and a contraction transformation along a specific axis. Notably, following discharge to 3.3&nbsp;V, the characteristic diffraction peak characteristic of the Na<sub>2</sub>V<sub>6</sub>O<sub>16</sub> phase vanishes, reverting to the Na<sub>2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub> phase. The manifestation of a diffraction peak akin to the charging state during the process of charging to 4&nbsp;V and discharging to 1.5&nbsp;V implies that the crystal structure remains unaltered pre and post Na<sup>+</sup> removal. Furthermore, it is imperative to highlight that the corresponding peak shift during the reinsertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into the lattice is entirely reversible, denoting an adaptable volume change and superb reversibility in the extraction/insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>.</p>
         
         <p>To assess the Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics in CVF and NVO, GITT measurements were conducted during charge/discharge processes (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0007">7a</a>). This analysis aimed to elucidate the impact of the multistep sodiation/desodiation reactions in CVF on ion diffusion and conductivity properties. The calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) for CVF during charging/discharging processes ranged from approximately 10<sup>−9</sup> to 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, significantly exceeding the <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> values for NVO (approximately 10<sup>−10</sup> to 10<sup>−13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>). This enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in CVF, attributed to the Ca and F co-doping, results in improved rate capability and faster charge-transfer kinetics.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202401481-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7673f379-0093-4777-810c-988c17aac0b6/aenm202401481-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7673f379-0093-4777-810c-988c17aac0b6/aenm202401481-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e2f12205-4bbb-4fed-a1ea-4262990920bf/aenm202401481-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7673f379-0093-4777-810c-988c17aac0b6/aenm202401481-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202401481-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202401481" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) GITT curves of CVF and NVO. b) Nyquist patterns and&nbsp;<i>Z</i><sub>re</sub>&nbsp;versus ω<sup>−1/2</sup>&nbsp;lines of NVO, CVO, NVF and CVF. c) Migration path of Na<sup>+</sup> for CVF.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <div class="paragraph-element">Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0007">7b</a> showcases the Nyquist plots and the equivalent circuit used for fitting of NVO, CVO, NVF and CVF to further delineate discharge and charge behaviors. The Nyquist plots exhibited by both electrodes, portray a distinct and discernible pattern characterized by a depressed semicircle in the high-frequency domain and a linear slope in the low-frequency domain. The distance from the origin to the zero point along the curve corresponds to the solution resistance (<i>R</i><sub>s</sub>), a parameter influenced significantly by the properties of the electrolyte and separator membrane. Noteworthy are the presence of constant phase elements (CPE) representing non-ideal capacitance arising from surface irregularities. The diameter of the depressed semicircle in the high-frequency domain indicates the impedance attributed to charge transfer at the electrode interface (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), while the sloping line in the low-frequency domain delineates the Warburg diffusion process occurring within the electrode (W).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0012_R_d8813743e1283" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Mater. Chem. A">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is evident that the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value for CVF (475 Ω) is substantially lower than that of NVO (1143 Ω), suggestive of a more expedited reaction kinetics pertaining to sodium ion diffusion at the electrode–electrolyte interfaces of CVF. Additionally, the Warburg factor (<i>σ</i>), which is associated with the slope of the linear fits in the low-frequency region as described below:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0038_R_d8813743e1294" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Electrochim. Acta">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>
