                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>wherein, <i>ω</i> represents the frequency, <i>R</i> represents the gas constant, <i>T</i> signifies the absolute temperature, <i>n</i> denotes the quantity of electrons transferred per molecule during the redox reaction, <i>A</i> represents the electrode surface area, <i>F</i> is the Faraday constant and <i>C</i> signifies the concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> within the electrolyte. The calculation of <i>D</i> yielded values of 2.43 × 10<sup>−13</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> for CVF and 7.36 × 10<sup>−15</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> for NVO, indicative of the beneficial influence of Ca and F doping on decreasing water content within the crystal structure, thereby augmenting the diffusion kinetics and subsequently enhancing the specific capacity. The fast Na diffusion kinetics demonstrated by CVF can be attributed to the easy diffusion paths (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0007">7c</a>) and low diffusion barriers of Na ions in the Ca and F co-doped channels (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0005">5e</a>).</div>
         
         <p>The observed voltage plateaus at 3.4 and 2.4&nbsp;V in the charge/discharge profiles align well with the redox peaks identified in the cyclic voltammetry CV curve, providing a comprehensive understanding of the electrochemical performance of CVF. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0008">8a</a> presents the CV profiles of NVO, CVO, NVF, and CVF over a sweep speed range of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> to 1.5–4.0&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). Notably, CVF exhibits a significantly higher peak redox current and expanded integral area compared to the other samples, indicating a substantial enhancement in specific capacity and redox reaction kinetics due to the incorporation of Ca and F dopants. The CV plots reveal the emergence of two prominent redox peak pairs across all four samples. CVF displays peaks at 2.4/2.57&nbsp;V and 3.36/3.51&nbsp;V, CVO at 2.34/2.63&nbsp;V and 3.34/3.48&nbsp;V, NVF at 2.32/2.65&nbsp;V and 3.33/3.52&nbsp;V, and NVO at 2.32/2.7&nbsp;V and 3.34/3.44&nbsp;V, typically attributed to the extraction and insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> within the NVO lattice matrix. Interestingly, the 2.4&nbsp;V reduction peak of the CVF splits into a 1.9&nbsp;V peak, potentially related to the Ca doping, which may partially convert vanadium to a +4 oxidation state, leading to a self-doping effect.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202401481-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/67498c3c-7ef1-495d-87b1-af9770ea1958/aenm202401481-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/67498c3c-7ef1-495d-87b1-af9770ea1958/aenm202401481-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/24dc8239-f431-4da4-b173-9ae914371aa0/aenm202401481-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/67498c3c-7ef1-495d-87b1-af9770ea1958/aenm202401481-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202401481-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202401481" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV profiles of NVO, CVO, NVF and CVF at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Capacitive contribution (orange shaded region) of CVF acquired at 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. c) Capacitive contribution (orange shaded region) of NVO at 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. d) CV profiles of CVF at varied scan speeds from 0.1 to 1.0 mVs<sup>−1</sup>. e) Linear relationship of log(<i>i</i>) versus log(<i>v</i>) plots for cathodic and anodic peaks of CVF. f) Percentage of measured pseudocapacitive contribution at 0.1 to 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> for CVF. g) CV curves of NVO at varied scan speeds from 0.1 to 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. h) Linear relationship of log(<i>i</i>) versus log(<i>v</i>) plots for cathodic and anodic peaks of NVO. i) Percentage of measured pseudocapacitive contribution at 0.1 to 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> for NVO.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0008">8b,c</a> depict the pseudocapacitance fitting diagrams of CVF and NVO at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The presence of asymmetric redox peaks in the CV graphs is indicative of sluggish kinetics, while CVF displays reduced asymmetry and accelerated kinetics compared to the other samples. To further probe the kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> storage for CVF and NVO, multi-sweep rate CV curves were obtained. The CV curves acquired at varying scan rates from 0.1 to 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0008">8d,g</a>. As the sweep speed increases, the primary oxidation peaks shift to higher potential regions, while the reduction peaks shift to lower potential regions.</p>
         
         <p>The relationship between peak current (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) and sweep speed (<i>v</i>) can be quantified using the formula <i>i</i><sub>p</sub> = <i>av<sup>b</sup></i>, where <i>a</i> value of <i>b</i> closer to 0.5 indicates an ionic diffusion-controlled process, while a value closer to 1.0 signifies a capacitive nature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0023_R_d8813743e1518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The b values for peaks I, II, III, and IV of CVF are all greater than 0.5 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0008">8e</a>), while the b values for peaks I, II, III, and IV of NVO are also greater than 0.5 and closer to 1.0, suggesting that the electrochemical reaction in both materials is influenced by a combination of diffusion and capacitive behavior (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0008">8h</a>). The linear relationships of the <i>i</i><sub>p</sub> versus square root of scan rate (<i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>) are presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information). The results suggest that the electrochemical behavior of CVF is more heavily influenced by diffusion due to the co-doping of Ca and F. CVF exhibits pseudo-capacitance contributions of 67.8%, 75.9%, 78.5%, 82.5%, and 89.7% at 0.1, 0.3, 0.5, 0.8, and 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0008">8f</a>). These contributions are smaller than those observed for NVO (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0008">8i</a>).</p>
         
         <p>To comprehensively evaluate the electrochemical performance of Ca and F co-doped vanadate materials, HC materials, renowned for their superior electrical conductivity, were selected as the negative electrode. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a,b</a> (Supporting Information) shows the morphologies of the HC negative electrodes. The micrographs reveal that HC possesses a large specific surface area and expanded lattice layer spacing, making it an ideal candidate for negative electrode materials. EDS analysis confirms the presence of N, O, and C in the crystal structure (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16c–f</a>, Supporting Information). The microstructure of HC can be characterized as a combination of rumpled and twisted graphene sheet fragments consisting of sp<sup>2</sup> hybridized carbon.</p>
         
         <p>These co-doped materials were subsequently integrated into a full cell for performance evaluation. The cycling performance and GCD profiles are presented in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0009">9a,b</a>. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0009">9a</a> shows that the initial discharge capacity of the material is approximately 65 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 400&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, consistent with the rate performance results observed in the half-cell tests (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0006">6a</a>). After 100 cycles, the capacity gradually decreases to approximately 40 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. This observation is further supported by the excellent electrochemical properties of HC, as evidenced in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0009">9c,d</a>. The synthesized HC anode exhibits exceptional rate performance and cycling properties, delivering capacities of 307, 275, 250, 216 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.8, and 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0009">9c</a>). Notably, it achieves a capacity of 203&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> with outstanding cycle stability (initial specific capacity of 161 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and a capacity retention rate of 99% after 1000 cycles) as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202401481-fig-0009">9d</a>. It is important to note that the electrochemical performance of the full cell is significantly influenced by the electrolyte. Therefore, global optimization of the full cell is crucial to further enhance its electrochemical performance.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202401481-fig-0009"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bcdc6f39-2c2b-4d0e-8c17-494f15c1fb03/aenm202401481-fig-0009-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/bcdc6f39-2c2b-4d0e-8c17-494f15c1fb03/aenm202401481-fig-0009-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/010bdd7c-070b-45cb-a044-059e61485195/aenm202401481-fig-0009-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bcdc6f39-2c2b-4d0e-8c17-494f15c1fb03/aenm202401481-fig-0009-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 9<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202401481-fig-0009&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202401481" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cycle performance diagram of CVF//HC. b) GCD graph of CVF//HC. c) Rate performance diagram of HC. d) Cycle performance diagram of HC.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>While layered P2 and O3 metal oxides have garnered considerable attention as cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0041_R_d8813743e1624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Appl. Ceram. Technol. Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a>-<a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0044_R_d8813743e1624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Mater. Today">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the sodium vanadate cathode exhibits a compelling advantage in terms of its superior rate performance and higher specific capacity. This enhanced performance stems from the unique structural characteristics of vanadate, which allow for rapid Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport and a higher theoretical capacity compared to traditional layered oxides. However, despite its promising rate capability, the sodium vanadate cathode faces limitations in long-term cycling performance. This is primarily attributed to structural instabilities arising from repeated Na<sup>+</sup> ion intercalation and deintercalation processes. While lattice regulation strategies can mitigate some of these issues, further improvements are necessary to achieve optimal cycling stability.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202401481-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202401481-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, a novel Ca/F co-doping approach has been proposed to enhance the electrochemical properties of layered NVO cathode materials. Through the utilization of DFT calculations, the impacts of Ca and F doping on cell volume, conductivity, and diffusivity have been thoroughly examined. The experimental results demonstrate a substantial increase in specific capacity following the co-doping of Ca and F. Specifically, the CVF cathode material exhibits initial specific discharge capacities of 220 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, 178&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, 132&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, and 114&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, and 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. At a current density of 200&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the initial specific discharge capacity is noted as 138&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, with a remarkable capacity retention rate of 76% after 100 cycles. Notably, both charge transfer impedance and diffusion impedance witness a significant decrease. The simulation results indicate a reduction in bandgap and an increase in conductivity post Ca/F co-doping. Additionally, the volume shrinkage effect during charging is mitigated, while the stronger bond energy of the Ca-O bond enhances structural stability. The co-doping of Ca/F allows for an expanded sodium layer spacing that reduces the energy barrier for sodium ion migration, facilitating rapid double continuous ion and electron transport and improving specific capacity. In essence, the modification strategy of Ca/F co-doping proves to be an effective means of enhancing stability, capacity, and rate performance of NVO layered Vanadate, thereby offering a new avenue for the preparation and exploration of sodium storage materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202401481-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202401481-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202401481-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202401481-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202401481-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202401481-sec-0070-title"> Sample Preparation</h4>
                  
                  <p>The Na<sub>2</sub>CaV<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>F cathode material was prepared through a straightforward one-step hydrothermal process. Initially, 10&nbsp;mmol of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> was delicately dispersed in 40&nbsp;mL of deionized water and stirred magnetically. Subsequently, 1.5&nbsp;mmol of NaF, 0.25&nbsp;mmol of CaCl<sub>2</sub>, and 10&nbsp;mmol of NaOH were meticulously added one after the other, with continuous agitation for a duration of 2 h. The resulting homogenous solution was then carefully transferred to a 100&nbsp;mL polyethylene Teflon-lined reactor and heated in an oven at 180&nbsp;°C for a period of 12 h. After the slow cooling of the mixture to room temperature, the self-assembled orange-red sample was gently washed with deionized water and alcohol thrice, followed by vacuum drying at 60&nbsp;°C for another 12 h. Finally, the sample underwent sintering at 300&nbsp;°C for 2 h, resulting in the creation of CVF. Following a similar meticulous approach, the samples of Na<sub>2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub> (NVO), Na<sub>2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>-(<i>x</i>%Ca) (CVO), and Na<sub>2</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>6</sub>-(<i>y</i>%F) (NVF) were all systematically prepared using the hydrothermal method (<i>x</i> = 1, 2.5 4; <i>y</i> = 10, 15, 20; % refers to the percentage of the doped element relative to sodium).</p>
                  
                  <p>The synthesis process of hard carbon (HC) is as follows: 6.0&nbsp;g of dicyanamide (DCDA) and 0.6&nbsp;g of homophenyltrionic acid were dissolved in 30&nbsp;mL of ethanol solution. The mixture was stirred magnetically for 36 h at room temperature to ensure a homogeneous solution. Second, the obtained solution was dried in an oven at 80&nbsp;°C for 24 h. Finally, the dried mixture was ground into a powder and placed into a tubular furnace. The furnace was heated to 800&nbsp;°C at a rate of 5&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup> under a nitrogen atmosphere, and maintained at 800&nbsp;°C for 2 h.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202401481-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202401481-sec-0080-title"> Material Characteristics</h4>
                  
                  <p>The morphology and size of the as-prepared composites were investigated utilizing field emission scanning electron microscope (FESEM, SU-70) and transmission electron microscope (TEM, F30). X-ray diffraction (XRD) measurements were conducted using a Bruker D8-A25 instrument and a Fullprof software was carried out to refine the lattice parameters. High resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM) was used to observe the internal structure of the crystal and measure the spacing of lattice and obtain selective area electron diffraction (SAED) image. The elemental distribution mapping and line scanning images were determined by energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS, Bruker Quantax). The valence of the elements was examined using X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Axis Ultra DLD). Fourier-transform infrared (FT-IR) spectra were obtained using Nicolet is 10. Thermogravimetric (TG) analysis was carried out on a Setsys Evolution 18 instrument at a heating rate of 10&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup> under an N<sub>2</sub> environment. Ultraviolet-visible spectrum was used to measure the bandgap of the samples (UV–Vis spectroscopy, Shimadzu UV-2550).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202401481-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202401481-sec-0090-title"> Electrochemical Performance Test</h4>
                  
                  <p>The composite cathode was prepared by aluminum foil as the collector fluid, active material, and superconductive black (SP), binder (PVDF: NMP = 1:19 mass ratio) at a ratio of 7:2:1. The electrolyte, a solution of 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in EC/DMC/EMC (v:v = 1:1) with 5% FEC, was carefully mixed. The electrode utilized was sodium sheet, while a GF/D glass fiber served as the diaphragm. In a controlled environment within an argon-filled glove box (water/oxygen: &lt;0.1&nbsp;ppm), electrochemical experiments were conducted in CR2025 cells. The electrochemical workstation (CHI660C) was utilized to obtain electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) and cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves. The Land CT2001A system and NEWARE system were employed to assess galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD), ex situ XRD test, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique test (GITT), cycle stability, and rate capability at varying rates. The EIS spectra were measured at a cell voltage of 2.56&nbsp;V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, with a signal amplitude of 5&nbsp;mV in a frequency range from 100&nbsp;kHz to 0.01&nbsp;Hz.</p>
                  
                  <p>In order to analyze the electrochemical performance of the doped sample more comprehensively, it was assembled into a full battery with a hard carbon anode and tested the charge-discharge performance. The assembly process is the same as that of a half battery.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="aenm202401481-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="aenm202401481-sec-0100-title"> DFT Calculations</h4>
                  
                  <p>In this study, the computations were carried out utilizing density functional theory (DFT) implemented in the Vienna Ab Initio Simulation Package (VASP) for all spin-polarized calculations.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0045_R_d8813743e1766" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Phys. Rev. B: Condens. Matter Mater. Phys.">45</a>, <a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0046_R_d8813743e1766" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Phys. Rev. B">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The ion-electron interaction was characterized employing the projector-augmented wave method (PAW),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0047_R_d8813743e1771" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Phys. Rev. B">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> wherein the valence electrons were described using a plane wave basis set with a kinetic energy cut-off of 600&nbsp;eV to ensure a converged total energy of 1 meV per atom. The electron exchange-correlation energy was treated with the generalized gradient approximation (GGA) incorporating the Perdew-Burke-Ernzerhof (PBE) functionals,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0048_R_d8813743e1776" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with electronic and ionic convergence criteria set at 10<sup>−6</sup>&nbsp;eV and 10<sup>−2</sup>&nbsp;eV Å<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. To account for van der Waals interactions, Grimme's scheme empirical correction (DFT-D3) was applied.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0049_R_d8813743e1788" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Energy Technol.">49</a>, <a href="#aenm202401481-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202401481-bib-0050_R_d8813743e1788" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Chem. Phys.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Brillouin zone was sampled using a Gamma-centered 2 × 3 × 2 Monkhorst-Pack grid for structural optimizations, and a 3 × 4 × 3 Monkhorst-Pack grid for static calculations. The investigation of minimum energy pathways was performed utilizing the climbing image nudged elastic band method (CI-NEB) with a path threshold of 0.02&nbsp;eV Å<sup>−1</sup>, and insertion points were determined based on the displacement of the diffusing atom.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>

    window.addEventListener('load', () => {
        document.querySelectorAll('.admin-bar-popup-menu ul li')?.forEach(function (li) {
